overview of lawIntl

description::
">international law:
International law is a set of rules and principles that govern the relationships between sovereign states and other international actors, such as international organizations and individuals. It is a complex and dynamic field that encompasses various legal frameworks, treaties, conventions, customary practices, and principles.

Key elements of international law include:
1. **Sources of International Law:**
- **Treaties:** Agreements between states that are binding under international law.
- **Customary International Law:** Practices that have developed among states and are accepted as legally binding.
- **General Principles of Law:** Legal principles common to various legal systems.
- **Judicial Decisions and Scholarly Writings:** These are considered subsidiary sources of international law.

2. **Subjects of International Law:**
- **States:** The primary subjects of international law.
- **International Organizations:** Entities like the United Nations, International Court of Justice, etc.
- **Individuals:** Increasingly, individuals are recognized as having certain rights and responsibilities under international law.

3. **State Sovereignty:**
- The principle of state sovereignty is a fundamental aspect of international law, emphasizing the equality of states and their right to govern their internal affairs without external interference.

4. **International Human Rights Law:**
- Protects the rights and dignity of individuals globally. Key documents include the Universal Declaration of Human Rights and various conventions.

5. **International Humanitarian Law:**
- Governs the conduct of armed conflict, aiming to protect civilians and combatants who are no longer taking part in hostilities.

6. **International Environmental Law:**
- Addresses global environmental challenges and promotes sustainable development.

7. **International Criminal Law:**
- Deals with crimes of an international concern, such as genocide, war crimes, and crimes against humanity.

8. **Use of Force and Peaceful Settlement of Disputes:**
- The United Nations Charter regulates the use of force among states and promotes peaceful settlement of disputes.

9. **International Courts and Tribunals:**
- Entities like the International Court of Justice (ICJ) adjudicate disputes between states, while other tribunals focus on specific issues like trade or human rights.

10. **Diplomatic and Consular Law:**
- Regulates the conduct of states and their representatives in their diplomatic and consular relations.

It's important to note that enforcement mechanisms in international law are often weaker compared to domestic legal systems. Compliance relies heavily on the willingness of states to adhere to their international obligations. The field continues to evolve as new challenges arise in the global community."

subject of lawIntl

description::
">subjects of international-law:
The subjects of international law are the entities that have the capacity to bear rights and obligations under international law. These entities include:
* **States:** States are the primary subjects of international law. They are defined as independent political entities that have control over a defined territory and population. States have the capacity to enter into treaties, to make war and peace, and to send and receive diplomatic representatives.
* **International organizations:** International organizations are entities that are created by states to cooperate on matters of common interest. These organizations can be either governmental or non-governmental. They have the capacity to enter into treaties, to make decisions that are binding on their member states, and to enjoy certain privileges and immunities.
* **Non-state entities:** Non-state entities are entities that are not states or international organizations. These entities can include individuals, corporations, and other legal entities. Non-state entities can have the capacity to bear rights and obligations under international law, but their capacity is generally more limited than that of states or international organizations. For example, individuals can be brought to trial for international crimes, but they cannot enter into treaties.
* **Special case entities:** Special case entities are entities that do not fit neatly into the categories of states, international organizations, or non-state entities. These entities can include the Holy See, the Order of Malta, and certain other entities that have been recognized as subjects of international law by the international community.

The status of subjects of international law is important because it determines which entities can be held responsible for violations of international law. It also determines which entities can enjoy the rights and privileges that are protected by international law.

The traditional view of the subjects of international law was that only states could be subjects of international law. However, in recent years, there has been a growing recognition that other entities, such as international organizations, non-state entities, and individuals, can also be subjects of international law. This recognition is reflected in the development of new bodies of international law, such as international human rights law and international criminal law.

The development of the concept of the subjects of international law is an ongoing process. As the international community becomes more complex, new entities will emerge that may need to be recognized as subjects of international law."

oznLawIntl.ICJ!=International-Court-of-Justice

description::
">International-court-of-justice:
The International Court of Justice (ICJ), also known as the World Court, is the principal judicial organ of the United Nations (UN). It settles disputes between states in accordance with international law and gives advisory opinions on legal questions referred to it by authorized United Nations organs and specialized agencies.

**Jurisdiction**
The ICJ has jurisdiction to hear cases between states that have accepted its jurisdiction. States may accept the ICJ's jurisdiction by making a declaration under Article 36 of the ICJ Statute or by agreeing to its jurisdiction in a specific case. The ICJ may also hear cases between states that are parties to a treaty that provides for the settlement of disputes by the ICJ.

**Procedure**
Cases before the ICJ are initiated by the filing of a written application with the Registry of the Court. The application must be filed by a state that is a party to the dispute. Once the application has been filed, the Registry of the Court sends a copy of it to the other state or states that are parties to the dispute. The other state or states then have a period of time to file a written reply to the application.
Once the pleadings have been exchanged, the ICJ may hold a hearing on the case. The hearing is an opportunity for the parties to present their arguments to the Court in person. The Court may also ask questions of the parties and their representatives.
After the hearing, the ICJ closes the case for deliberation. The Court then deliberates in private and reaches a decision by a majority vote. The decision of the Court is final and binding on the parties to the dispute.

**Advisory opinions**
The ICJ may also give advisory opinions on legal questions referred to it by authorized United Nations organs and specialized agencies. These opinions are not binding, but they are influential sources of international law.

**The role of the ICJ**
The ICJ plays an important role in the international legal system. It is a court of last resort for states that are unable to resolve their disputes through other means. The ICJ's decisions help to develop and clarify international law. The Court's advisory opinions also provide guidance to the United Nations and its specialized agencies.
The ICJ is a permanent and independent court. Its judges are elected by the United Nations General Assembly and the Security Council. The judges are independent of their governments and serve on the Court for a term of nine years.
The ICJ is located in The Hague, Netherlands. The Court's official languages are English and French.

**Conclusion**
The ICJ is an important institution in the international legal system. It plays a vital role in the settlement of international disputes and the development of international law. The Court's work helps to promote peace and justice in the world.

jurisdiction of Unicj

description::
">jurisdiction of International-Court-of-Justice
The International Court of Justice (ICJ) is the principal judicial organ of the United Nations. It is the only international court with jurisdiction over states to resolve legal disputes between them. The ICJ's jurisdiction is both contentious and advisory.

**Contentious Jurisdiction**
The ICJ's contentious jurisdiction is exercised in accordance with international law and allows states to bring legal disputes against each other. There are two main ways for a state to refer a dispute to the ICJ:
1. **Voluntary submission:** A state can agree to submit a particular dispute to the ICJ by signing a special agreement, known as a compromis, with the other state involved in the dispute.
2. **Declarations under Article 36(2) of the Statute:** States may also make a declaration recognizing the compulsory jurisdiction of the ICJ for certain categories of disputes. This means that they agree to submit to the ICJ's jurisdiction without having to sign a special agreement each time a dispute arises.
The ICJ's contentious jurisdiction is limited to disputes between states. It cannot hear cases between individuals or non-state entities. Additionally, the ICJ's jurisdiction is based on consent, meaning that both parties to a dispute must agree to submit it to the Court for adjudication.

**Advisory Jurisdiction**
The ICJ's advisory jurisdiction is exercised at the request of authorized United Nations organs or specialized agencies. The Court is asked to provide an opinion on legal questions that are relevant to the work of the requesting body. Advisory opinions are not binding, but they can be influential in shaping international law.
In order for the ICJ to exercise its advisory jurisdiction, the following conditions must be met:
1. **The requesting body must be authorized to make the request:** Only the Security Council, the General Assembly, and certain specialized agencies are authorized to request an advisory opinion from the ICJ.
2. **The question put to the ICJ must be of a legal nature:** The question must be capable of being resolved by the application of legal principles and rules.
3. **The question must not be of a political nature:** The question must not be primarily concerned with matters of policy or expediency.
4. **The question must not be one that the Court is not already seized of, or that the Court has already decided in a previous advisory opinion:** The Court will not provide an advisory opinion on a question that it has already ruled on, or that is pending before the Court in contentious proceedings.
The ICJ's advisory jurisdiction has been used to address a wide range of legal questions, including the interpretation of treaties, the legality of international acts, and the status of individuals in international law. Advisory opinions can be a valuable tool for clarifying international law and promoting its development.

In summary, the International Court of Justice has two main types of jurisdiction: contentious and advisory. Contentious jurisdiction allows states to bring legal disputes against each other, while advisory jurisdiction allows the Court to provide opinions on legal questions at the request of authorized United Nations organs or specialized agencies."

oznLawIntl.ICC!=International-Criminal-Court

description::
">International-Criminal-Court:
The International Criminal Court (ICC) is the first and only permanent, treaty-based international criminal court that investigates and prosecutes individuals accused of the most serious crimes of international concern, namely genocide, crimes against humanity, war crimes, and the crime of aggression. The ICC is a court of last resort, meaning it can only intervene if national courts are unwilling or unable to genuinely prosecute these crimes.

**Here are some of the key facts about the ICC:**
* **Established:** 2002
* **Headquarters:** The Hague, Netherlands
* **Number of member states:** 123
* **Jurisdiction:** The ICC can investigate and prosecute crimes committed on the territory of a member state, or by a national of a member state.

**The ICC has investigated and prosecuted crimes in a number of countries, including:**
* **Africa:** Democratic Republic of the Congo, Sudan, Uganda, Central African Republic, Cτte d'Ivoire, Kenya, Mali, Libya, Burundi, and South Sudan
* **Asia:** Afghanistan, Iraq, Georgia, and the Philippines
* **Europe:** Bosnia and Herzegovina, Serbia, Kosovo, Georgia, and Ukraine
* **Americas:** Colombia, Guatemala, and Honduras

**The ICC has faced a number of challenges, including:**
* **Limited cooperation from some states:** Some states, such as the United States, are not members of the ICC and have not cooperated with its investigations.
* **Resource constraints:** The ICC is a relatively new institution and has limited resources. This has made it difficult to investigate and prosecute all of the cases that it has been referred.
* **Lack of enforcement power:** The ICC does not have its own police force or prisons. It relies on member states to cooperate with its arrest warrants and to enforce its sentences.

Despite these challenges, the ICC has made a significant contribution to the fight against impunity for the most serious crimes of international concern. It has brought justice to victims, deterred future crimes, and helped to strengthen the rule of law.

descriptionLong::
"The International Criminal Court (ICC or ICCt)[2] is an intergovernmental organization and international tribunal that sits in The Hague, Netherlands. The ICC is the first and only permanent international court with jurisdiction to prosecute individuals for the international crimes of genocide, crimes against humanity, war crimes, and the crime of aggression. It is intended to complement existing national judicial systems and it may therefore exercise its jurisdiction only when national courts are unwilling or unable to prosecute criminals. The ICC lacks universal territorial jurisdiction, and may only investigate and prosecute crimes committed within member states, crimes committed by nationals of member states, or crimes in situations referred to the Court by the United Nations Security Council.
The ICC began operations on 1 July 2002, upon the entry into force of the Rome Statute, a multilateral treaty that serves as the court's foundational and governing document. States which become party to the Rome Statute become members of the ICC, serving on the Assembly of States Parties, which administers the court. As of December 2020, there are 123 ICC member states; 42 states have neither signed nor become parties to the Rome Statute."
[{2021-02-26} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Criminal_Court]

evoluting of oznIcc

description::
">evolution of international-criminal-court:
The evolution of the International Criminal Court (ICC) can be traced back to the atrocities committed during World War II, which sparked the establishment of the Nuremberg and Tokyo Tribunals. These tribunals, while groundbreaking in their own right, were ad hoc courts limited to specific conflicts and perpetrators. The desire for a permanent international criminal court emerged to address the most serious crimes of international concern, regardless of where or when they were committed.

In 1948, the United Nations General Assembly adopted the Convention on the Prevention and Punishment of the Crime of Genocide, marking a significant step towards establishing an international criminal justice system. However, the idea of a permanent ICC remained elusive for decades due to political and legal challenges.

A breakthrough came in 1998 when 120 states adopted the Rome Statute of the International Criminal Court, the legal basis for establishing the ICC. The Rome Statute defined the ICC's jurisdiction, powers, and procedures, and established the crimes that the ICC could prosecute.

The ICC officially came into force on July 1, 2002, with 60 states becoming parties to the Rome Statute. Since then, the ICC has evolved into a crucial institution in the pursuit of international criminal justice.

Key milestones in the evolution of the ICC include:
1. **Establishment of the Ad Hoc Committee on the Establishment of an International Criminal Court (1995):** This committee was tasked with preparing a draft statute for an ICC, which eventually formed the basis for the Rome Statute.

2. **Adoption of the Rome Statute (1998):** The adoption of the Rome Statute marked a major turning point, establishing the legal framework for the ICC.

3. **Entry into force of the Rome Statute (2002):** With the ratification of the Rome Statute by 60 states, the ICC officially came into existence.

4. **Opening of the ICC's headquarters in The Hague (2003):** The ICC established its permanent headquarters in The Hague, Netherlands.

5. **Issuance of the first arrest warrant (2006):** The ICC issued its first arrest warrant for Thomas Lubanga Dyilo, a Congolese warlord accused of recruiting and using child soldiers.

6. **First convictions (2012):** The ICC handed down its first convictions against Thomas Lubanga Dyilo and Germain Katanga, both Congolese warlords.

7. **Opening of investigations in new regions (2015-present):** The ICC expanded its investigations to include situations in Latin America, Asia, and the Middle East.

8. **Adoption of the crime of aggression (2017):** The ICC amended its Rome Statute to include the crime of aggression, a significant step in addressing the most serious crime under international law.

The ICC continues to evolve, facing challenges and opportunities as it strives to fulfill its mandate of upholding international criminal justice. The court's ability to investigate and prosecute the most serious crimes of international concern, regardless of where or when they were committed, has set a precedent for international criminal justice.
Sources
books.google.com/books?id=MHTQZPDckk0C"

enforcement of lawIntl

description::
">enforcement of international-law:
The enforcement of international law is a complex and multifaceted issue. Unlike domestic law, which is enforced by a centralized authority with a monopoly on violence, international law is enforced through a decentralized system of self-help, cooperation, and institutions. This means that there is no single body or mechanism that is responsible for enforcing international law, and the effectiveness of enforcement varies depending on the specific norm, the parties involved, and the circumstances of the case.
**Self-help** is the most common method of enforcing international law. This means that states take action themselves to ensure that other states comply with international law. Self-help measures can be diplomatic, such as protest or suspension of relations, or they can be economic, such as sanctions or boycotts. In some cases, states may even resort to the use of force to enforce international law.
**Cooperation** is also an important factor in the enforcement of international law. States often work together to enforce international law through treaties, international organizations, and other multilateral mechanisms. For example, the United Nations Security Council can authorize the use of force to enforce international law, and the International Criminal Court can prosecute individuals for serious violations of international law.
**Institutions** play a crucial role in the enforcement of international law. These institutions can provide a forum for states to resolve disputes, monitor compliance with international law, and impose sanctions on states that violate international law. Some of the most important international institutions for the enforcement of international law include the International Court of Justice, the World Trade Organization, and the International Monetary Fund.

The enforcement of international law is often challenging, but it is essential for maintaining a rules-based order in the international system. Without effective enforcement, international law would be nothing more than a set of empty promises.

Here are some of the specific methods that are used to enforce international law:
* **Diplomatic measures:** These measures include protest, suspension of relations, and diplomatic isolation.
* **Economic measures:** These measures include sanctions, boycotts, and asset freezes.
* **Judicial measures:** These measures involve bringing cases against states or individuals who have violated international law.
* **Use of force:** This is a measure of last resort and is only authorized under international law in very limited circumstances.

The effectiveness of international law enforcement varies depending on the specific norm, the parties involved, and the circumstances of the case. Some norms are more easily enforced than others, and states are more likely to comply with international law when they are powerful and have a strong interest in the norm being enforced.

In recent years, there have been a number of initiatives to strengthen the enforcement of international law. These initiatives include the creation of new international courts and tribunals, the development of new enforcement mechanisms, and the increased use of sanctions and other punitive measures.

Despite these efforts, the enforcement of international law remains a challenge. This is due to a number of factors, including the decentralized nature of the international system, the reluctance of states to use force, and the lack of a centralized enforcement authority. However, there is a growing recognition of the importance of international law, and there is a renewed commitment to strengthening its enforcement."
[{2023-11-25 retrieved} https://bard.google.com/chat/e9634735c3cd95c9?hl=en&pli=1]

description::
">evolution of international-law:
Sure, here is a summary of the evolution of international law:

**Early Origins**
The roots of international law can be traced back to ancient civilizations, where treaties and customary practices governed interactions between states. However, it was in Europe that international law truly began to develop, driven by the need to manage relations between increasingly interconnected states.

**Emergence of Modern International Law**
The Peace of Westphalia, concluded in 1648, marked a turning point in the development of international law. The treaty established the principle of state sovereignty, which laid the foundation for the modern international legal system.

**Hugo Grotius and the Rise of Natural Law**
Hugo Grotius, a Dutch jurist, is widely considered the father of international law. His seminal work, "De Jure Belli ac Pacis" (On the Law of War and Peace), published in 1625, laid out the foundations of natural law, which posited that international law was derived from universal moral principles.

**Positivism and the Rise of State Consent**
In the 19th century, positivism emerged as the dominant theory of international law. Positivists argued that international law was not based on natural law but rather on the consent of states. This shift reflected the growing importance of state sovereignty and the development of treaties as the primary source of international law.

**The Hague Conventions and the Codification of International Law**
The Hague Conventions, held in 1899 and 1907, were a landmark in the codification of international law. The conventions addressed a range of issues, including the conduct of warfare and the protection of neutral states.

**The League of Nations and the Rise of International Institutions**
The establishment of the League of Nations in 1920 marked a new era in international law. The League was the first permanent international organization dedicated to maintaining peace and security. It also played a role in codifying international law through conventions and treaties.

**The United Nations and the Post-World War II Era**
The United Nations, established in 1945, replaced the League of Nations and became the central forum for international cooperation and lawmaking. The UN Charter enshrines key principles of international law, such as the prohibition of the use of force and the peaceful settlement of disputes.

**Contemporary Developments in International Law**
International law has continued to evolve in the post-Cold War era, addressing new challenges such as globalization, environmental protection, and human rights. International institutions, such as the World Trade Organization and the International Criminal Court, have also emerged to play an increasingly important role in upholding international law.

**Key Themes in the Evolution of International Law**
The evolution of international law has been shaped by several key themes:
* **Sovereignty:** The principle of state sovereignty has been central to the development of international law.
* **Consent:** The principle of state consent is the basis for the creation of international obligations.
* **Codification:** The codification of international law into treaties and conventions has provided greater clarity and certainty.
* **Institutionalization:** International institutions have played an increasingly important role in upholding international law.
* **Emerging Challenges:** International law is constantly evolving to address new challenges, such as globalization, environmental protection, and human rights.

**Conclusion**
International law has played a crucial role in promoting cooperation, maintaining peace, and protecting human rights in the global arena. As the world continues to face complex and interconnected challenges, international law will remain an essential tool for managing relations between states and upholding the rule of law."
[{2023-11-24 retrieved} https://bard.google.com/chat/44fb3cfeb1893386?hl=en&pli=1]

">evolution of international-law:
The evolution of international law is a complex and dynamic process that has taken place over centuries. It has been shaped by historical events, changes in state practices, and the development of new norms and institutions. Here is a brief overview of key stages in the evolution of international law:

1. **Ancient and Medieval Periods (3000 BCE – 1648 CE):**
- During ancient times, various civilizations had systems of customary laws governing relations between states and individuals.
- In medieval Europe, the Catholic Church played a significant role in shaping laws and norms, particularly through the concept of "just war" and the idea of a natural law governing human conduct.

2. **Peace of Westphalia (1648):**
- The Peace of Westphalia is often considered a foundational moment for modern international law. It ended the Thirty Years' War and marked the beginning of the state-centric system.
- The principle of state sovereignty emerged, emphasizing non-intervention in the domestic affairs of sovereign states.

3. **18th and 19th Centuries:**
- Emergence of the law of nations and the development of the idea of international legal personality for states.
- The publication of legal scholars such as Emer de Vattel contributed to the codification and understanding of international law principles.

4. **Hague Conferences (1899 and 1907):**
- The conferences aimed to codify laws of war and establish the Permanent Court of Arbitration, reflecting a growing interest in international cooperation and dispute resolution.

5. **League of Nations (1920):**
- The League of Nations was the first international organization with the goal of maintaining peace and preventing conflicts.
- It contributed to the development of international law through treaties and agreements.

6. **Post-World War II Era:**
- The United Nations (UN) was established in 1945, with its Charter serving as a foundational document of modern international law.
- The Nuremberg Trials set important precedents for prosecuting individuals for war crimes and crimes against humanity.

7. **Cold War and Decolonization:**
- The Cold War era saw a focus on arms control and the development of treaties addressing issues such as nuclear weapons and the law of the sea.
- Decolonization led to the creation of new states, influencing the development of international legal norms.

8. **Contemporary Period:**
- The end of the Cold War marked a shift in international relations, with increased emphasis on human rights, humanitarian intervention, and the responsibility to protect (R2P).
- International criminal tribunals, such as the International Criminal Court (ICC), were established to prosecute individuals for genocide, war crimes, and crimes against humanity.

9. **Globalization and New Challenges:**
- Globalization has brought about new challenges, including issues related to the environment, cybersecurity, and transnational crime.
- Efforts to address these challenges involve the development of new legal frameworks and mechanisms.

The evolution of international law continues as the international community grapples with contemporary issues and strives to adapt legal norms to the changing global landscape. Ongoing developments include efforts to strengthen international institutions, enhance accountability, and address emerging threats to peace and security."
[{2023-11-24 retrieved} https://chat.openai.com/c/2dd89370-9b8e-448c-9137-a580a2b323d6]

specific-tree-of-lawIntl::
">types of international-law:
International law encompasses a wide range of legal principles and norms that govern the relations between sovereign states and other international actors. It can be classified into several types based on the nature and scope of the rules. Here are some key types of international law:

1. **Public International Law:**
- **Law of Peace:** Governs relations between states during times of peace.
- **Law of War (International Humanitarian Law):** Regulates conduct during armed conflict.

2. **Private International Law (Conflict of Laws):**
- Deals with issues related to individuals and entities that have connections to multiple legal jurisdictions.

3. **Treaty Law:**
- Governs the creation, interpretation, and enforcement of international agreements between states and international organizations.

4. **Customary International Law:**
- Based on consistent state practice coupled with a belief that such practice is required by law (opinio juris).

5. **Jus Cogens:**
- Peremptory norms of international law that are considered fundamental and must be upheld by all states. Examples include prohibitions against genocide and slavery.

6. **Human Rights Law:**
- Protects the rights and dignity of individuals and groups, often enshrined in international treaties and declarations.

7. **International Criminal Law:**
- Addresses individuals' criminal responsibility for certain offenses of international concern, such as genocide, war crimes, and crimes against humanity.

8. **Environmental Law:**
- Focuses on the protection of the environment and sustainable development at the international level.

9. **Law of the Sea:**
- Governs the use of the world's oceans, including issues such as territorial waters, exclusive economic zones, and the rights and responsibilities of states in maritime zones.

10. **Space Law:**
- Regulates activities in outer space, including the use and exploration of celestial bodies.

11. **International Economic Law:**
- Includes rules governing international trade, investment, and economic relations between states.

12. **Diplomatic and Consular Law:**
- Regulates the conduct of diplomatic and consular relations between states.

13. **State Responsibility:**
- Addresses the legal consequences of a state's internationally wrongful acts and the responsibility for reparations.

14. **International Organizations Law:**
- Governs the establishment, functions, and responsibilities of international organizations like the United Nations.

These categories are not mutually exclusive, and there is often overlap between them. Additionally, the development and interpretation of international law are influenced by the decisions of international courts and tribunals, as well as the opinions of legal scholars and practitioners."
[{2023-11-27 retrieved} https://chat.openai.com/c/00cf4920-7687-4a23-b3cd-e58c3c9c356a]

">types of international-law:
International law can be broadly divided into two main categories: public international law and private international law.

**Public international law** is concerned with the relationships between states. It governs such matters as diplomacy, war, and trade. Some of the most important sources of public international law are treaties, customary international law, and general principles of law.
**Private international law** is concerned with the relationships between private individuals and entities in different countries. It governs such matters as contracts, torts, and family law. Some of the most important sources of private international law are treaties, custom, and general principles of law.

Here are some of the specific areas of international law:
* **International economic law** is concerned with the economic relations between states. It governs such matters as trade, investment, and finance.
* **International security law** is concerned with the use of force by states. It governs such matters as war, self-defense, and nuclear weapons.
* **International criminal law** is concerned with the prosecution of individuals for crimes such as genocide, war crimes, and crimes against humanity.
* **International environmental law** is concerned with the protection of the environment. It governs such matters as pollution, climate change, and biodiversity.
* **Diplomatic law** is concerned with the conduct of relations between states. It governs such matters as immunity, privileges, and ceremonies.
* **Human rights law** is concerned with the protection of the human rights of individuals. It governs such matters as freedom of expression, freedom of association, and the right to life.
* **International humanitarian law** is concerned with the protection of individuals during armed conflict. It governs such matters as the use of force, the treatment of prisoners of war, and the protection of civilians.
* **International law of the sea** is concerned with the use of the oceans. It governs such matters as fishing, navigation, and pollution.
* **Space law** is concerned with the use of outer space. It governs such matters as the exploration of space, the use of satellites, and the militarization of space."

lawIntl.human-rights

description::
">human-rights international-law:
International human rights law is the body of international law that deals with the protection and promotion of human rights at the international, regional, and domestic levels. It is a complex and evolving field of law that draws on a variety of sources, including treaties, customary international law, general principles of law, and soft law.

**The foundation of international human rights law is the Universal Declaration of Human Rights (UDHR),** which was adopted by the United Nations General Assembly in 1948. The UDHR is a non-binding declaration that sets out a broad range of civil, political, economic, social, and cultural rights that all human beings are entitled to.

**The UDHR has been supplemented by a number of other treaties,** including the International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights (ICCPR) and the International Covenant on Economic, Social, and Cultural Rights (ICESCR). These treaties are binding on states that have ratified them, and they set out more detailed obligations for states to protect and promote human rights.

**International human rights law also includes a number of customary international law norms,** which are rules of international law that are based on longstanding state practice and opinio juris (the belief that the rule is law). Customary international law norms include the prohibition of torture, genocide, and crimes against humanity.

**In addition to treaties and customary international law, international human rights law also includes a number of soft law instruments,** such as declarations, resolutions, and guidelines. Soft law instruments are not binding on states, but they can still have a significant impact on the development of international human rights law.

**International human rights law is enforced through a variety of mechanisms,** including international courts, tribunals, and human rights monitoring bodies. These mechanisms can investigate allegations of human rights violations and make recommendations to states to improve their human rights records.

**International human rights law is a complex and evolving field of law, but it is also a vitally important one.** It plays a crucial role in protecting the human rights of all people, regardless of their nationality, race, gender, religion, or any other status.

Here are some of the key principles of international human rights law:
* **Universality:** Human rights are universal, meaning that they apply to all people, everywhere, without discrimination.
* **Indivisibility:** Human rights are indivisible, meaning that they cannot be ranked or prioritized. All human rights are equally important.
* **Interdependence:** Human rights are interdependent, meaning that they are all interconnected and reinforce each other.
* **Non-discrimination:** All human beings are entitled to all human rights without discrimination on any grounds, such as race, color, sex, language, religion, political or other opinion, national or social origin, property, birth or other status.
* **Equality:** All human beings are born free and equal in dignity and rights.
* **Accountability:** States are accountable for their actions or omissions in relation to human rights.

lawIntl.sea

description::
">sea international law:
The **law of the sea**, also known as the **maritime law**, is a body of customs, treaties, and international agreements that govern activities and legal relationships on the sea. It aims to establish order, maintain peaceful relations, and promote the sustainable use of marine resources.

**Key principles of the law of the sea** include:
1. **Freedom of navigation:** All ships, regardless of their flag, have the right to innocent passage through territorial waters. This means that they can pass through these waters without stopping or communicating with the coastal state, as long as they do not engage in any activities that could threaten the peace and security of the coastal state.
2. **Exclusive economic zones (EEZs):** Coastal states have exclusive rights to explore, exploit, conserve, and manage the natural resources within their EEZs, which extend up to 200 nautical miles from their coastlines.
3. **Deep seabed resources:** The deep seabed, beyond national jurisdiction, is the common heritage of mankind. This means that its resources are owned by all of humanity and must be exploited for the benefit of all. The International Seabed Authority (ISA) was established to regulate the exploitation of deep seabed resources.
4. **Protection of the marine environment:** The law of the sea requires states to take measures to protect the marine environment from pollution and other harmful activities. This includes preventing marine pollution from ships, land-based sources, and atmospheric sources.
5. **Peaceful uses of the sea:** The law of the sea prohibits the use of the sea for hostile purposes, such as the threat or use of force. It also establishes rules for the settlement of maritime disputes.

The **primary source of the law of the sea** is the **United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea** (UNCLOS), which was adopted in 1982 and entered into force in 1994. UNCLOS is the most comprehensive and widely accepted treaty on the law of the sea.

In addition to UNCLOS, there are a number of other treaties and agreements that govern specific aspects of maritime law, such as fishing, conservation, and maritime transportation.

The law of the sea is a dynamic and evolving field of law. As new uses are developed for the sea, and as the marine environment changes, the law of the sea must adapt to meet these new challenges.

Here are some of the **recent developments in the law of the sea:**
* The increasing importance of **transboundary marine resources**, such as fish stocks and marine mammals, is leading to calls for greater international cooperation in their management.
* The growing problem of **marine pollution** is prompting states to adopt stricter regulations and enforcement measures.
* The development of new technologies, such as deep seabed mining, is raising concerns about the impact on the marine environment and the distribution of benefits.

The law of the sea plays a crucial role in ensuring that the oceans are used sustainably and peacefully for the benefit of all humanity. As the world becomes more interconnected, the need for a strong and effective legal framework for the sea is becoming increasingly important."
[{2023-11-29 retrieved} https://bard.google.com/chat/5b772b2e663db011?hl=en&pli=1]

lawIntl.space

description::
">space international-law:
International space law, also known as space law, is a framework of international treaties and custom that governs the exploration, use, and protection of outer space. These laws were developed to ensure that space activities are conducted in a safe, responsible, and sustainable manner for the benefit of all humankind.

**Key Principles of International Space Law**
* **Non-appropriation of outer space:** No country or entity may claim sovereignty over any part of outer space, including the Moon and other celestial bodies.
* **Peaceful use of outer space:** Outer space is to be used for peaceful purposes only, and the threat or use of force is prohibited.
* **Free access to all areas of celestial bodies:** All countries have the right to explore and use the Moon and other celestial bodies without discrimination.
* **Cooperation:** States are encouraged to cooperate in the exploration and use of outer space for the benefit of all.
* **Liability for damage caused by space objects:** States are responsible for any damage caused by their space objects in orbit or on the ground.
* **Protection of the environment:** States are responsible for preventing the contamination of outer space and celestial bodies.

**Major International Space Treaties**
* **Treaty on Principles Governing the Activities of States in the Exploration and Use of Outer Space, including the Moon and Other Celestial Bodies (Outer Space Treaty)**: This treaty, which entered into force in 1967, is the cornerstone of international space law. It establishes the basic principles of non-appropriation, peaceful use, and free access to outer space.
* **Agreement on the Rescue of Astronauts, the Return of Astronauts and the Return of Objects Launched into Outer Space (Rescue Agreement)**: This treaty, which entered into force in 1968, provides for the rescue and return of astronauts and spacecraft in distress.
* **Convention on International Liability for Damage Caused by Space Objects (Liability Convention)**: This treaty, which entered into force in 1972, establishes a system of liability for damage caused by space objects.
* **Convention on Registration of Objects Launched into Outer Space (Registration Convention)**: This treaty, which entered into force in 1975, requires states to register all space objects launched from their territory.
* **Moon Agreement:** This treaty, which has not yet entered into force, prohibits the militarization of the Moon and other celestial bodies and provides for the equitable sharing of resources extracted from these bodies.

**Challenges in International Space Law**
As space exploration and technology advance, new challenges are emerging for international space law. These challenges include:
* **The commercialization of space:** The increasing involvement of private companies in space activities is raising questions about the application of international space law to these activities.
* **The exploration and exploitation of natural resources in outer space:** The potential for mining resources from asteroids and other celestial bodies is raising concerns about the equitable distribution of these resources and the environmental impact of their extraction.
* **The militarization of space:** The development of new weapons systems that could be used in space is a concern for many states and international organizations.

International space law is constantly evolving as new challenges arise. The United Nations Committee on the Peaceful Uses of Outer Space (COPUOS) is the main forum for international cooperation on space law. UNOOSA, the United Nations Office for Outer Space Affairs, provides administrative and technical support to COPUOS and other UN activities related to space law.

The future of international space law is uncertain, but it is clear that it will play an increasingly important role in ensuring that space activities are conducted in a safe, responsible, and sustainable manner for the benefit of all humankind.

lawIntl.state-responsibility

description::
">state responsibility international-law:
State responsibility is a branch of international law that deals with the legal consequences of a state's breach of an international obligation. The principles of state responsibility are designed to ensure that states are held accountable for their actions and that victims of breaches are compensated for the harm they have suffered.

**The Draft Articles on State Responsibility**, adopted by the International Law Commission (ILC) in 2001, are the most authoritative statement of the principles of state responsibility. The Articles define an internationally wrongful act as a conduct consisting of a breach of an international obligation by a state. The Articles also set out the conditions under which a state is responsible for an internationally wrongful act and the consequences of such an act.

**The main principles of state responsibility are:**
* **Attribution:** A state is responsible for an internationally wrongful act only if the act is attributable to it. This means that the act must have been committed by the state or by a person or entity that is acting on the state's behalf.
* **Breach of an international obligation:** A state is responsible for an internationally wrongful act only if it breaches an international obligation. This means that the state must have a legal duty to act or refrain from acting in a certain way.
* **Injury:** A state is responsible for an internationally wrongful act only if the act causes injury to another state or to its nationals. This means that the act must have caused some harm or loss to the victim.

**The consequences of an internationally wrongful act are:**
* **Cessation of the wrongful act:** The state responsible for the internationally wrongful act must cease the act if it is continuing.
* **Reparation:** The state responsible for the internationally wrongful act must make reparation for the injury caused by the act. This means that the state must compensate the victim for the harm or loss that it has suffered.
* **Satisfaction:** The state responsible for the internationally wrongful act must give satisfaction to the victim. This means that the state must acknowledge the breach and express regret for the harm caused.

**The principles of state responsibility are important because they:**
* **Hold states accountable for their actions:** The principles ensure that states are not able to act with impunity and that they must take responsibility for their breaches of international law.
* **Protect the rights of states and their nationals:** The principles provide a framework for states to seek redress for breaches of international law and to compensate victims for the harm they have suffered.
* **Promote the rule of law:** The principles contribute to the development of a stable and orderly international legal system.

**State responsibility is a complex and evolving area of international law. The principles of state responsibility are constantly being developed and refined by international courts and tribunals, by the ILC, and by scholars.**"

lawIntl.war

description::
">international law of war:
International humanitarian law (IHL), also known as the law of armed conflict, is a body of law that regulates the conduct of hostilities and seeks to limit the suffering caused by armed conflict. IHL applies to all parties to an armed conflict, whether they are states or non-state actors.

The main principles of IHL are:
* **Humanity:** Civilians and combatants who are no longer taking part in hostilities must be treated humanely. This means that they must not be killed, tortured, or subjected to other forms of cruel, inhuman, or degrading treatment.
* **Distinction:** Civilians and civilian objects must be distinguished from combatants and military objectives. Attacks must be directed only at military objectives, and civilians must not be deliberately targeted.
* **Proportionality:** The expected military advantage of an attack must outweigh the expected civilian harm. Attacks that are likely to cause excessive civilian harm are prohibited.
* **Precautions in attack:** Parties to an armed conflict must take all feasible precautions to minimize civilian harm. This includes taking measures to avoid civilian casualties, such as giving advance warning of attacks.

IHL is based on a number of treaties and conventions, including the Geneva Conventions, the Hague Conventions, and the Additional Protocols to the Geneva Conventions. These treaties and conventions have been ratified by most countries in the world.

IHL is enforced by a number of bodies, including the International Committee of the Red Cross (ICRC), the United Nations Security Council, and national courts. The ICRC is a humanitarian organization that works to protect the lives and dignity of people affected by armed conflict. The United Nations Security Council has the power to authorize the use of force and to impose sanctions on countries that violate IHL. National courts can prosecute individuals for war crimes, which are serious violations of IHL.

IHL is an important tool for protecting civilians and limiting the suffering caused by armed conflict. It is a complex body of law, but it is essential for ensuring that armed conflicts are conducted in a humane manner."
[{2023-11-28 retrieved} https://bard.google.com/]

lawIntl.customary

description::
"Customary international law is an aspect of international law involving the principle of custom. Along with general principles of law and treaties, custom is considered by the International Court of Justice, jurists, the United Nations, and its member states to be among the primary sources of international law.
Many governments accept in principle the existence of customary international law, although there are differing opinions as to what rules are contained in it.
In 1950, the International Law Commission listed the following sources as forms of evidence of customary international law: treaties, decisions of national and international courts, national legislation, opinions of national legal advisors, diplomatic correspondence, and practice of international organizations.[1] In 2018, the Commission adopted Conclusions on Identification of Customary International Law with commentaries.[2] The United Nations General Assembly welcomed the Conclusions and encouraged their widest possible dissemination.[3]"
[{2020-11-27} https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Customary_international_law]

lawIntl.treaty

description::
">international treaty:
An **international treaty**, or **international agreement**, is a legal instrument that establishes a formal relationship between two or more states or international organizations. Treaties are governed by international law and are binding on the parties to the treaty. They are an important tool for regulating international relations and promoting cooperation among states.

**Key characteristics of international treaties:**
* **Written form:** Treaties are typically written documents that are signed and ratified by the parties involved.
* **Governed by international law:** Treaties are subject to the rules of international law, which are set out in treaties and other international instruments.
* **Binding on the parties:** Treaties create legal obligations for the parties that are involved. These obligations must be fulfilled in good faith.

**Types of international treaties:**
* **Multilateral treaties:** These treaties are between three or more states. For example, the United Nations Charter is a multilateral treaty.
* **Bilateral treaties:** These treaties are between two states. For example, the North American Free Trade Agreement (NAFTA) is a bilateral treaty.

**Purposes of international treaties:**
* **Promote peace and security:** Treaties can help to prevent wars and resolve conflicts between states. For example, the Treaty on the Non-Proliferation of Nuclear Weapons (NPT) is a treaty that aims to prevent the spread of nuclear weapons.
* **Encourage cooperation:** Treaties can promote cooperation between states on a range of issues, such as trade, the environment, and human rights. For example, the Paris Agreement is a treaty that aims to address climate change.
* **Resolve disputes:** Treaties can provide a framework for resolving disputes between states. For example, the International Court of Justice (ICJ) is a court that can hear disputes between states that are based on treaties.

**Examples of important international treaties:**
* **Treaty of Westphalia (1648):** This treaty ended the Thirty Years' War and established the modern system of sovereign states.
* **Convention on the Law of the Sea (1982):** This treaty governs the use of the oceans and establishes a legal framework for maritime navigation and resource exploitation.
* **Universal Declaration of Human Rights (1948):** This declaration sets out the fundamental rights and freedoms that all people are entitled to.
* **Paris Agreement (2015):** This treaty aims to limit global warming to well below 2 degrees Celsius, preferably to 1.5 degrees Celsius, compared to pre-industrial levels.

**International treaties play a vital role in maintaining international peace and security, promoting cooperation and resolving disputes between states. They are an essential part of the international legal system and have a significant impact on the lives of people around the world.**

lawIntl.Hague-conventions-{1899-1907}

description::
">Hague-conventions:
The Hague Conventions are a series of multilateral treaties and declarations negotiated at two international peace conferences held at The Hague in the Netherlands in 1899 and 1907. The conventions codified the laws and customs of war, making them the first comprehensive attempt to regulate armed conflict.

The conventions established a number of important principles, including:
* The obligation to distinguish between combatants and non-combatants
* The prohibition of indiscriminate attacks
* The protection of civilians and civilian objects
* The treatment of prisoners of war
* The prohibition of certain weapons

The Hague Conventions have been widely ratified and have become part of customary international law. They have been instrumental in preventing the escalation of violence and protecting civilians during armed conflicts.
Here are some of the key Hague Conventions:
* **Hague Convention (I) for the Pacific Settlement of International Disputes (1899)**: This convention established the Permanent Court of Arbitration, the first international court for the peaceful settlement of disputes.
* **Hague Convention (II) for the Reduction of Armaments** (1899): This convention called for the limitation of the size of armies and navies.
* **Hague Convention (III) on the Opening of Hostilities** (1899 and 1907): This convention specifies the formal declaration of war and bans surprise attacks.
* **Hague Convention (IV) on the Laws and Customs of War on Land** (1899 and 1907): This convention establishes rules governing the conduct of hostilities on land, including the prohibition of indiscriminate attacks, the protection of civilians and civilian objects, and the treatment of prisoners of war.
* **Hague Convention (VI) on the Status of Enemy Merchant Ships at the Outbreak of Hostilities** (1907): This convention provides for the protection of merchant ships belonging to belligerents from capture by the enemy during the first two weeks of a war.
* **Hague Convention (IX) on Bombardment by Naval Forces in Time of War** (1907): This convention restricts the use of naval bombardment against coastal towns and villages, requiring that such attacks be directed against military objectives.

The Hague Conventions have been instrumental in promoting international cooperation and preventing the escalation of violence. They remain of great importance today, as they provide a framework for regulating armed conflict and protecting civilians."
[{2023-11-28 retrieved} https://bard.google.com/chat/3f74272008015f1c?hl=en&pli=1]

lawIntl.Geneva-conventions-{1864}

description::
">Geneva conventions:
The Geneva Conventions are a series of four international treaties and three additional protocols that establish international legal standards for humanitarian treatment in war. They were first adopted in 1864, and have been revised and expanded several times since then. The Geneva Conventions apply in all international and non-international armed conflicts, and they protect people who are not taking part in the hostilities (civilians, medics, and aid workers), as well as those who are no longer participating in the hostilities, such as wounded, sick, and shipwrecked soldiers and prisoners of war.

**Key Principles of the Geneva Conventions**
* **Humanity:** The Geneva Conventions are based on the principle of humanity, which means that they aim to protect the lives and dignity of individuals during armed conflict.
* **Impartiality:** The Geneva Conventions must be applied impartially, without discrimination of any kind, such as race, color, religion, sex, birth, or wealth.
* **Neutrality:** The International Committee of the Red Cross (ICRC), the leading organization responsible for upholding the Geneva Conventions, must remain neutral in all its activities.

**Protections Under the Geneva Conventions**
The Geneva Conventions provide a range of protections for people affected by armed conflict, including:
* **Protection from violence and abuse:** Civilians, medical personnel, and aid workers must be protected from violence and abuse, including torture, cruel, inhuman, or degrading treatment.
* **Access to medical care:** All wounded and sick people, without distinction, must be given access to medical care.
* **Preservation of cultural property:** Cultural property, such as museums, libraries, and religious buildings, must be protected from destruction or damage.
* **Repatriation of prisoners of war:** Prisoners of war must be released and repatriated at the end of hostilities.

**Challenges to Implementing the Geneva Conventions**
Despite their importance, the Geneva Conventions face a number of challenges in being implemented fully. These challenges include:
* **Violations of the Conventions:** Parties to armed conflicts often violate the Geneva Conventions, either intentionally or unintentionally.
* **Lack of accountability:** There is often a lack of accountability for violations of the Geneva Conventions, which makes it difficult to deter future violations.
* **Difficulties in accessing conflict zones:** It can be difficult for humanitarian organizations to access conflict zones to provide assistance to those who need it.

Despite these challenges, the Geneva Conventions remain an essential framework for protecting human life and dignity during armed conflict. They are an important reminder that even in the midst of war, there are basic standards of humanity that must be upheld.

lawIntl.convention

description::
">international convention vs treaty:
The terms "international convention" and "treaty" are often used interchangeably, but there is a subtle difference between the two.

**Legally, there is no difference between an international convention and a treaty.** Both are legally binding agreements between states that are governed by international law. They are entered into for a variety of purposes, such as regulating international trade, establishing diplomatic relations, or protecting human rights.

**The main difference between the two terms is that "convention" is typically used for formal multilateral treaties with a broad number of parties, while "treaty" is a more general term that can be used for any kind of international agreement, regardless of the number of parties involved.** For example, the United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea is a multilateral treaty with 168 parties, while the Paris Agreement on climate change is a multilateral treaty with 196 parties.

In practice, the terms "convention" and "treaty" are often used interchangeably, and there is no need to be pedantic about which term to use. However, if you are writing a formal document, it is always best to use the term that is most appropriate for the context.

Here is a table summarizing the key differences between international conventions and treaties:
| Feature | International Convention | Treaty |
| **Used for** | Formal multilateral treaties with a broad number of parties | Any kind of international agreement, regardless of the number of parties involved |
| **Tendency** | Used for treaties that deal with important or sensitive issues | Used for all types of treaties |
| **Examples** | United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea | Paris Agreement on climate change |"
[{2023-11-28 retrieved} https://bard.google.com/chat/31fb1aceb40eb17e?hl=en&pli=1]

lawIntl.general-principles-of-law

description::
">general principles of international-law:
General principles of international law are fundamental norms that are recognized and accepted by the international community of states. They are considered to be part of customary international law, which is a source of international law that is based on long-standing and generally accepted practices of states. General principles of international law are often used to fill gaps in the law or to interpret other sources of international law.

Some of the most important general principles of international law include:
* **Sovereign equality of states:** All states are equal and independent, and no state has the right to interfere in the internal affairs of another state.
* **Pacta sunt servanda:** Treaties must be observed in good faith.
* **Nullus crimen sine lege:** There is no crime without law.
* **Aut in deroga aut non:** A state cannot rely on its own internal law to justify a breach of international law.
* **Res judicata:** A matter that has been judicially decided cannot be relitigated.
* **Estoppel:** A state is bound by its own representations.
* **Good faith:** States must act in good faith in their relations with other states.
* **Equity:** States must act in a fair and equitable manner.
* **Justice:** States must uphold justice in their relations with other states.

General principles of international law are an important source of international law and are used to promote the rule of law in the international system. They provide a framework for states to interact with each other in a peaceful and cooperative manner.

Here are some examples of how general principles of international law have been applied:
* In the case of the *Interhandel Case*, the International Court of Justice (ICJ) held that the principle of pacta sunt servanda required Switzerland to comply with a treaty it had signed with the United States, even though the Swiss Federal Council had declared the treaty null and void.
* In the case of the *Fisheries Jurisdiction Case*, the ICJ held that the principle of good faith required Iceland to negotiate with the United Kingdom in order to reach an agreement on fishing quotas, even though Iceland had not violated any specific international law provision.
* In the case of the *Oil Platforms Case*, the ICJ held that the principle of estoppel prevented Iran from relying on its own internal law to justify its seizure of oil platforms owned by the United States.

General principles of international law are a dynamic and evolving source of international law. They are constantly being developed and refined by the international community of states."

lawIntl.judicial-dicisions

description::
">judicial-dicisions of international-law:
Judicial decisions play a significant role in international law, serving as a secondary source of law and providing valuable insights into the interpretation and application of international legal norms. While not formally binding on subsequent disputes, judicial decisions are often persuasive and can shape the development of international law in various ways.

**Primary and Secondary Sources of International Law**
International law is primarily derived from treaties, customary international law, and general principles of law. These sources are considered primary sources, as they directly establish legal norms. Judicial decisions, on the other hand, are categorized as secondary sources, meaning they interpret and apply primary sources to specific cases.

**Role of Judicial Decisions in International Law**
Despite their secondary status, judicial decisions hold considerable influence in international law. They serve several key functions:
1. **Evidence of Customary International Law:** Judicial decisions can provide evidence of state practice, opinio juris (belief in the legal bindingness of a norm), and the general acceptance of a customary rule.
2. **Interpretation of Treaties:** Courts and tribunals play a crucial role in interpreting treaty provisions, resolving ambiguities, and determining the scope of treaty obligations.
3. **Development of International Law:** Judicial decisions can contribute to the development of international law by identifying new legal principles, expanding the scope of existing norms, and refining the interpretation of international legal instruments.

**Persuasive Value of Judicial Decisions**
The persuasive value of judicial decisions varies depending on the court or tribunal issuing the decision, the quality of its reasoning, and the consistency with other judicial decisions. Decisions from courts with higher authority, such as the International Court of Justice (ICJ), carry greater weight than those from lower courts or specialized tribunals.

**Stare Decisis in International Law**
Unlike national legal systems that adhere to the principle of stare decisis, which requires courts to follow precedents set by higher courts, international law does not have a formal doctrine of stare decisis. However, courts and tribunals often give significant deference to the decisions of higher courts and influential tribunals, particularly when they address similar legal issues.

**Impact of Judicial Decisions**
Judicial decisions can have a significant impact on the international legal landscape, affecting the behavior of states, influencing the interpretation of international legal norms, and shaping the development of international law. While not formally binding, judicial decisions often set precedents, guide state practice, and contribute to the overall evolution of international law."

">scolarly-writings of international-law:
Scholarly writings of international law are a vital source of information and analysis for scholars, practitioners, and policymakers alike. They provide a wealth of insights into the development, interpretation, and application of international law.

**Types of scholarly writings**
There are many different types of scholarly writings in international law, including:
* **Articles:** Articles are short, focused pieces of scholarship that typically analyze a specific legal issue or problem. They are typically published in academic journals or edited collections.
* **Books:** Books are longer, more comprehensive works of scholarship that typically provide a more in-depth analysis of a particular topic or area of international law.
* **Casebooks:** Casebooks are collections of judicial decisions that are accompanied by commentary and analysis from scholars. They are used in law schools to teach students about international law.
* **Commentaries:** Commentaries are detailed analyses of specific international treaties or other legal instruments. They provide line-by-line commentary on the text of the instrument and discuss its interpretation and application.

**Sources of scholarly writings**
There are many different sources of scholarly writings in international law. Some of the most important sources include:
* **Academic journals:** Academic journals are peer-reviewed publications that publish original research in international law.
* **Edited collections:** Edited collections are collections of essays on a particular topic in international law that are edited by one or more scholars.
* **Book publishers:** Book publishers publish books on a wide range of topics in international law.
* **Law schools:** Law schools often have their own repositories of scholarly writings, including faculty working papers and student papers.
* **International organizations:** International organizations, such as the United Nations and the International Law Commission, publish a variety of scholarly writings on international law.

**Use of scholarly writings**
Scholarly writings are used for a variety of purposes, including:
* **Teaching:** Scholarly writings are used to teach students about international law in law schools and other educational settings.
* **Research:** Scholarly writings are used by researchers to develop new theories and insights about international law.
* **Practicing law:** Scholarly writings are used by lawyers to research legal issues and to develop arguments in court.
* **Policymaking:** Scholarly writings are used by policymakers to inform their decisions about international law.

**Importance of scholarly writings**
Scholarly writings are important for a number of reasons. They provide a wealth of information and analysis about international law, they help to develop new theories and insights about international law, and they inform the practice of international law. Scholarly writings are also essential for the development of international law as a discipline.
**Here are some of the most important scholarly writings in international law:**
* **The Law of Nations** by Hugo Grotius (1625)
* **De Jure Belli ac Pacis** by Grotius (1625)
* **On the Laws of War and Peace** by Emmerich de Vattel (1758)

* **New Directions in International Law** by W. Michael Reisman and James Crawford (1988)
* **The Sources of International Law** by Ian Brownlie (1990)
* **International Law** by James Crawford (2006)
* **The Future of International Law** by Martti Koskenniemi (2011)

These are just a few of the many important scholarly writings in international law. There is a vast body of scholarly literature on international law, and it continues to grow rapidly as the discipline evolves."

lawIntl.Universal-declaration-of-human-rights-(Unudhr)

info of Unudhr

description::
"The Universal Declaration of Human Rights (UDHR) is a milestone document in the history of human rights. Drafted by representatives with different legal and cultural backgrounds from all regions of the world, the Declaration was proclaimed by the United Nations General Assembly in Paris on 10 December 1948 (General Assembly resolution 217 A) as a common standard of achievements for all peoples and all nations. It sets out, for the first time, fundamental human rights to be universally protected and it has been translated into over 500 languages."
[https://www.un.org/en/universal-declaration-human-rights/]

Preamble

Whereas recognition of the inherent dignity and of the equal and inalienable rights of all members of the human family is the foundation of freedom, justice and peace in the world,

Whereas disregard and contempt for human rights have resulted in barbarous acts which have outraged the conscience of mankind, and the advent of a world in which human beings shall enjoy freedom of speech and belief and freedom from fear and want has been proclaimed as the highest aspiration of the common people,

Whereas it is essential, if man is not to be compelled to have recourse, as a last resort, to rebellion against tyranny and oppression, that human rights should be protected by the rule of law,

Whereas it is essential to promote the development of friendly relations between nations,

Whereas the peoples of the United Nations have in the Charter reaffirmed their faith in fundamental human rights, in the dignity and worth of the human person and in the equal rights of men and women and have determined to promote social progress and better standards of life in larger freedom,

Whereas Member States have pledged themselves to achieve, in co-operation with the United Nations, the promotion of universal respect for and observance of human rights and fundamental freedoms,

Whereas a common understanding of these rights and freedoms is of the greatest importance for the full realization of this pledge,

Now, Therefore THE GENERAL ASSEMBLY proclaims THIS UNIVERSAL DECLARATION OF HUMAN RIGHTS as a common standard of achievement for all peoples and all nations, to the end that every individual and every organ of society, keeping this Declaration constantly in mind, shall strive by teaching and education to promote respect for these rights and freedoms and by progressive measures, national and international, to secure their universal and effective recognition and observance, both among the peoples of Member States themselves and among the peoples of territories under their jurisdiction.

Article 1

All human beings are born free and equal in dignity and rights. They are endowed with reason and conscience and should act towards one another in a spirit of brotherhood.

* McsEngl.Unudhr'article-01,

Article 2

Everyone is entitled to all the rights and freedoms set forth in this Declaration, without distinction of any kind, such as race, colour, sex, language, religion, political or other opinion, national or social origin, property, birth or other status. Furthermore, no distinction shall be made on the basis of the political, jurisdictional or international status of the country or territory to which a person belongs, whether it be independent, trust, non-self-governing or under any other limitation of sovereignty.

Article 11

11.1 Everyone charged with a penal offence has the right to be presumed innocent until proved guilty according to law in a public trial at which he has had all the guarantees necessary for his defence.

11.2 No one shall be held guilty of any penal offence on account of any act or omission which did not constitute a penal offence, under national or international law, at the time when it was committed. Nor shall a heavier penalty be imposed than the one that was applicable at the time the penal offence was committed.

13.2 Everyone has the right to leave any country, including his own, and to return to his country.

16.2 Marriage shall be entered into only with the free and full consent of the intending spouses.

16.3 The family is the natural and fundamental group unit of society and is entitled to protection by society and the State.

Article 22

Everyone, as a member of society, has the right to social security and is entitled to realization, through national effort and international co-operation and in accordance with the organization and resources of each State, of the economic, social and cultural rights indispensable for his dignity and the free development of his personality.

23.2 Everyone, without any discrimination, has the right to equal pay for equal work.

23.4 Everyone has the right to form and to join trade unions for the protection of his interests.

25.2 Motherhood and childhood are entitled to special care and assistance. All children, whether born in or out of wedlock, shall enjoy the same social protection.

26.1 Everyone has the right to education. Education shall be free, at least in the elementary and fundamental stages. Elementary education shall be compulsory. Technical and professional education shall be made generally available and higher education shall be equally accessible to all on the basis of merit.

26.2 Education shall be directed to the full development of the human personality and to the strengthening of respect for human rights and fundamental freedoms. It shall promote understanding, tolerance and friendship among all nations, racial or religious groups, and shall further the activities of the United Nations for the maintenance of peace.

26.3 Parents have a prior right to choose the kind of education that shall be given to their children.

lawIntl.UN-convention-on-the-law-of-the-sea

description::
·

name::
* McsEngl.UNCLOS!=UN-convention-on-the-law-of-the-sea,
* McsEngl.lawIntl.017-UN-convention-on-the-law-of-the-sea!⇒Unclos,
* McsEngl.lawIntl.UN-convention-on-the-law-of-the-sea!⇒Unclos,

PREAMBLE

The States Parties to this Convention,

Prompted by the desire to settle, in a spirit of mutual understanding and cooperation, all issues relating to the law of the sea and aware of the historic significance of this Convention as an important contribution to the maintenance of peace, justice and progress for all peoples of the world,

Noting that developments since the United Nations Conferences on the Law of the Sea held at Geneva in 1958 and 1960 have accentuated the need for a new and generally acceptable Convention on the law of the sea,

Conscious that the problems of ocean space are closely interrelated and need to be considered as a whole,

Recognizing the desirability of establishing through this Convention, with due regard for the sovereignty of all States, a legal order for the seas and oceans which will facilitate international communication, and will promote the peaceful uses of the seas and oceans, the equitable and efficient utilization of their resources, the conservation of their living resources, and the study, protection and preservation of the marine environment,

Bearing in mind that the achievement of these goals will contribute to the realization of a just and equitable international economic order which takes into account the interests and needs of mankind as a whole and, in particular, the special interests and needs of developing countries, whether coastal or land-locked,
